Plant Sc. 6, 162 (2015)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d9165e1816">11</a></sup>. This reference genome-based GBS analysis identified 2150 (mean SNP map density 141.9âkb) and 2199 (138.7âkb) SNPs among the genotypes belonging to <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> cultivar groups, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep12806#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 15 and 16</a>). The slight variation in the SNP density and thus diversity between <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> is possibly due to a more recent secondary bottleneck in <i>kabuli</i> chickpea<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 240â246 (2013)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d9165e1840">28</a></sup>. A maximum variability and density of SNPs identified between the <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> genotypes specifically in 30â40âMb genomic region of chromosome 4 were observed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep12806#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>), which is agreed well with the previous documentation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 240â246 (2013)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d9165e1853">28</a></sup>. This highly variable genomic location could be a target region of interest for further understanding the domestication-led evolutionary patterns in <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> chickpea. Numerous informative genome-wide well-distributed genetic markers with high intra-specific polymorphic potential are required for marker-assisted genetic improvement of the chickpea genome with a narrow genetic base. In this context, two groups each of more than two thousands SNPs differentiating the <i>desi</i> genotypes and the <i>kabuli</i> genotypes identified in our study will be of significance in the construction of high-density integrated genetic and physical maps. This will be also useful for genetic/association mapping for efficient delineation of genes/QTLs governing important agronomic traits leading to genetic enhancement studies in chickpea. Moreover, 2150 and 2199 SNPs showing polymorphism within <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> groups was derived from 715 and 757 annotated protein-coding genes, respectively. These gene-based SNPs could be utilized for establishing rapid marker trait linkages and targeted mapping of genes harboring QTLs associated with important traits in chickpea. Collectively, the aforementioned outcomes reflect the broader applicability of the present <i>desi</i> genome assembly as a reference for multiple NGS-based low-scale resequencing and GBS-derived SNP mining and genotyping-related analyses in diverse <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> chickpea genotypes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep12806/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep12806/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep12806_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep12806/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep12806_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="427" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Distribution of SNPs within the <i>desi</i> group (39 desi genotypes) and the <i>kabuli</i> group (53 <i>kabuli</i> genotypes) of chickpea along the pseudomolecules (Ca_LG1-8).</p><p> Distribution of genes (green) is followed by the SNPs within the <i>desi</i> group and the <i>kabuli</i> group of chickpea geneotypes, respectively. Red rectangle denotes genomic region with maximum variation in chickpea genotypes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep12806/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>We have attempted to improve and advance the previously reported genome assembly of a <i>desi</i> type chickpea cultivar (ICC 4958) using an improved high throughput genetic marker map. Pseudomolecules corresponding to eight linkage groups were increased by 2.7 fold in size. Filling of sequence gaps amounting 27âMb with the newly generated short read data reduced N-content of the previous assembly by almost 50% and resulted in annotation of 30,257 protein-coding genes, 2686 more than the previous assembly and 1988 more than the CDC Frontier assembly. These genes accounted for about 95% of the chickpea gene space. The gene density analysis in the predicted pericentromeric regions suggested a low anchoring of repeat-rich sequences due to unavailability of genetic markers in these regions. This can only be improved by producing longer sequence reads and increasing scaffold sizes. As expected, the annotated protein-coding genes in both the chickpea genome assemblies displayed an overall sequence similarity. However, the lack of extensive sequence identity between ~30% genes may have resulted due to sequence errors or gaps and use of different annotation pipelines in the two assemblies. About 8% of all the CDSs in this assembly encoded for transcription factors. This is similar to the estimates in recently sequenced legume genomes, however, higher than those predicted in the <i>Medicago</i> and <i>Lotus japonicus</i>. Species-specific genes are originated due to gene duplication, recombination and movement of transposable elements and encode proteins with specialized functions for the new species. The most abundant conserved domains observed in the chickpea-specific genes had the signatures of transposable elements indicating that the genesis of at least some of these genes was due to movement of transposable elements. Divergence of <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> chickpea types about 8000 years ago as assessed through the rate of synonymous substitution is consistent with the proposition that the <i>kabuli</i> type was derived from the <i>desi</i> types after domestication on the basis of traits. This reference <i>desi</i> genome assembly could serve as an efficient genomic resource for mining SNPs at a genome-wide scale in diverse <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> genotypes and thus would expedite genomics-assisted breeding applications and genetic enhancement targeting multiple qualitative and quantitative traits in chickpea. Finally, a pair wise comparison between the pseudomolecules of the <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> chickpea assemblies displayed extensive local collinearity in spite of some large structural variations. These variations might be due to the use of different genetic maps in the two assemblies, which necessitates the creation of a reference chickpea genetic map. The apparent disagreement between the two chickpea genome assemblies would limit their use in the genetic improvement of the crop. Therefore, a further improvement with the help of larger sequence reads and physically mapped BAC library is necessary to produce a reference genome assembly of chickpea<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Integrated physical, genetic and genome map of chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.). Funct. Integr. Genomics 14, 59â73 (2014)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d9165e1969">50</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Sequencing and assembly</h3><p>DNA samples from the chickpea cultivar ICC 4958 were used to prepare genomic DNA libraries as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Jain, M. et al. A draft genome sequence of the pulse crop chickpea (Cicer arientinum L.). Plant J. 74, 715â729 (2013)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d9165e1985">27</a></sup>. The sequence reads reported in the previous draft assembly and the new MP reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep12806#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S1</a>) from two libraries of 20âkb insert size generated by 454/Roche GS FLX Titanium platform (<a href="http://www.454.com">http://www.454.com</a>) were assembled following the method described previously. 100 base long PE reads were generated using a whole genome shotgun library of insert size 320â620 bases through Illumna HiSeq1000 platform (Illumina Inc., San Diego, CA, USA). High-quality sequence reads were quality filtered using NGS toolkit and further used to fill the sequence gaps using SOAP <i>de novo</i> gap closure tool version 1.1 with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Patel, R. K. &amp; Jain, M. NGS QC Toolkit: A toolkit for quality control of next generation sequencing data. PLoS ONE 7, e30619 (2012)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2002">51</a></sup>. The sequence scaffolds were screened for mitochondrial, chloroplast, cloning vectors and bacterial DNA contamination following methods described before and additionally by NCBI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Bajaj, D. et al. A combinatorial approach of comprehensive QTL-based comparative genome mapping and transcript profiling identified a seed weight-regulating candidate gene in chickpea. Sci. Rep. 5, 9264 (2015)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2007">23</a></sup>. Finally, the scaffolds and contigs were anchored to corresponding linkage groups using marker sequences of a genetic map constructed with a biparental mapping population <i>C. arietinum</i> cv. ICC 4958âÃâ<i>C. reticulatum</i> cv. PI 489777 (<a href="http://nipgr.res.in/CGAP2/home.php">http://nipgr.res.in/CGAP2/home.php</a>). The assembly was submitted to NCBI under BioProject ID:PRJNA78951 with a submission ID:SUB713178. The newly generated reads were submitted to NCBI sequence read archive (SRA) database (SRR1632264, SRR1647666, â67, â69 and â70).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Gene annotation</h3><p>Repeat sequences were identified by software packages REPEATMODELLER, PILER, REPEATSCOUT and LTR_FINDER and masked by REPEATMASKER (http://repeatmasker.org) and REPEATPROTEIN MASK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Edgar, R. C. &amp; Myres, E. W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21 (Suppl), i152âi158 (2005)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2032">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Price, A.L., Jones, N.C. &amp; Pevzner, P.A. de novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21 (Suppl), i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2035">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. (2007) LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full- length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2038">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 25, 4.10.1â4.10.14 (2004)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2041">55</a></sup>. Repeat-masked genome assembly was used for annotating protein-coding genes by three approaches, <i>ab initio</i>, homology-based and EST-based. Augustus and GENESCAN tools with parameters trained on <i>Arabidopsis</i> were used for <i>ab initio</i> prediction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19 (Suppl), ii215âii225 (2003)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2054">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516â522 (2000)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2057">57</a></sup>. We used EXONERATE (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/~guy/exonerate/">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/~guy/exonerate/</a>) for homology-based prediction using protein sequences predicted in <i>kabuli</i> and <i>desi</i> chickpea draft assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Campbell, M.A. et al. Comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in rice and comparative analyses with Arabidopsis. BMC Genomics 7, 327 (2006)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2075">58</a></sup>. For EST-based approach, we aligned chickpea transcriptome reads for spliced alignment using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Garg, R. et al. Gene discovery and tissue-specific transcriptome analysis in chickpea with massively parallel pyrosequencing and web resource development. Plant Physiol. 156, 1661â1678 (2012)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2079">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Campbell, M.A. et al. Comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in rice and comparative analyses with Arabidopsis. BMC Genomics 7, 327 (2006)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2082">58</a></sup>. Outputs from these three approaches were integrated by EVIDENCEMODELLER (EVM) to generate consensus gene set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9 (R7), (2008)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2086">59</a></sup>. Finally, the EVM output was run through PASA for identification of spliced variants and prediction of untranslated regions and identified 30,649 gene models and 31,078 transcripts. We filtered out 392 gene models on the basis of small coding sequences (&lt;150 bases) and high N-content (ââ¥10%) finally to predict at 30,257 gene models and 30,686 CDSs. Genes encoding Ribosomal RNAs, transfer RNAs, micro RNAs and small nuclear RNAs were annotated following the previously described methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Jain, M. et al. A draft genome sequence of the pulse crop chickpea (Cicer arientinum L.). Plant J. 74, 715â729 (2013)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2091">27</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Functional annotation</h3><p>Functional annotation was done by sequence alignment with TAIR10 protein database using BLASTP with cut off E value &lt;1e-5. Proteins, which did not show significant similarity with TAIR database, were used to align with <i>M. truncatula</i> deduced proteome for functional annotation. Gene ontology terms were assigned using Blast2GO pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674â3676 (2005)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2106">60</a></sup>. Methods for identifying genes encoding transcription factor proteins and <i>R</i>-genes are described in the Result section. Tissue-specific gene expression in four tissues was performed using 454 RNAseq data described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Garg, R. et al. Gene discovery and tissue-specific transcriptome analysis in chickpea with massively parallel pyrosequencing and web resource development. Plant Physiol. 156, 1661â1678 (2012)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2113">20</a></sup>. Reads were quality-filtered using NGS toolkit. Quality filtered reads were mapped on annotated mRNA sequences using GSMapper (Roche Applied Sciences, <a href="http://www.roche-applied-science.com">http://www.roche-applied-science.com</a>) with default parameters. Uniquely mapped reads per million total reads (RPM) were used to generate expression profiles. To generate the tissue-specific expression profile, genes with 0âRPM value in all, but one tissue were considered. For tissue-preferential expression of transcription factor (TF)-encoding genes, RPM values of a gene in all four tissues were converted to fold expression values by normalizing with its highest RPM value, thus converting the highest RPM value of a gene to 1. Fold expression values of TF-encoding genes in four different tissues were plotted as heatmap. Tissue-preferential expression was considered if the expression of a gene in a particular tissue was at least three-fold higher than its expression in all other tissues.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Pair-wise sequence comparison and synteny</h3><p>Pair wise sequence comparison of pseudomolecules was performed using algorithms in SyMap v4.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Soderlund, C., Bomhoff, M. &amp; Nelson, W. M. SyMAP v3.4: a turnkey synteny system with application to plant genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, e68 (2011)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2132">61</a></sup>. A whole genome dot plot with eight linkage groups of <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> chickpea was generated by selecting hits with &gt;90% identity. Inter/intra-species gene synteny and collinearity were detected by MCScanX using default parameters considering pBLASTââ¤1e-5. Links of collinear blocks between sets of LGs/chromosomes are shown by dual-synteny plot or circle plot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and colineraity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2142">62</a></sup>. Peptide sequences of orthologous and paralogous gene-pairs were aligned using ClustalW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Chenna, R. et al. Multiple sequence alignment with the Clustal series of programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 3497â3500 (2003)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2146">63</a></sup>. The output alignment file and the corresponding CDS sequences were used to determine synonymous substitution rates (Ks) using PAL2NAL and CODEML in PAML 4.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D., &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequencing alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W609â612 (2006)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2151">64</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2154">65</a></sup>. Species divergence was calculated using formula Tâ=âKs/2r. The value of r was taken as 6.1âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup> per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The evolutionary fate and consequences of duplicate genes. Science 290, 1151â1155 (2000)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2160">66</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">SNP-mining</h3><p>GBS reads were quality-filtered using NGS tool kit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Deokar, A. A. et al. Genome wide SNP identification in chickpea for use in development of a high-density genetic map and improvement of chickpea reference genome assembly. BMC Genomics 15, 708 (2014)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2173">49</a></sup>. Reads from 39 desi and 53 kabuli genotypes were grouped separately and mapped on chickpea pseudomolecules using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kujur, A. et al. Employing genome-wide SNP discovery and genotyping strategy to extrapolate the natural allelic diversity and domestication patterns in chickpea. Front. Plant Sc. 6, 162 (2015)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2177">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/srep12806#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d9165e2180">67</a></sup>. The sam files were converted to bam files by using samtools and then to .vcf format to identify SNPs. Only unique non-reference allele supported by at least three reads was considered.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec18" data-title="Additional Information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec18-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec18">Additional Information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec18-content"><p><b>Accession codes</b> Genome assembly, annotation data and all the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep12806#MOESM1">supplementary figures</a>, tables, data are available for viewing and downloading at <a href="http://nipgr.res.in/CGAP2/home.php">http://nipgr.res.in/CGAP2/home.php</a> We will open it for public for view and download. User Id and password will not be required. Genome assembly and annotation are available at the National Centre for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) as Bioproject ID PRJNA78951. Additional sequence data generated for advanced draft assembly is submitted to NCBI (SRA) database (SRR1632264, SRR1647666, â67, â69 and â70).</p><p><b>How to cite this article</b>: Parween, S. <i>et al.</i> An advanced draft genome assembly of a <i>desi</i> type chickpea (<i>Cicer arietinum</i> L.). <i>Sci. Rep.</i> <b>5</b>, 12806; doi: 10.1038/srep12806 (2015).</p></div></div></section>
